Moisture can cause high voltage to track to ground through compromised spark plug wires, a cracked distributor cap or rotor, or a damaged coil, leading to misfires. The FSM lists 'Spark plug wires: Poor insulation, broken wires,' 'Distributor cap or rotor: Dirty or cracked,' and 'Coil: Damaged or malfunctioning' as causes for an engine running poorly at high RPM, which would include misfires. 'Spark plugs: Fouled, burned, cracked porcelain' are also listed.       "diagnostic_reasoning": "The symptom of the starter barely turning the engine or just clicking when hot, but working fine on a cold start, points to a heat-related issue. This could be due to:\n1.  **Excessive voltage drop in the starting circuit when hot:** Heat increases electrical resistance in wiring and connections. A marginal battery, corroded cables, or a faulty solenoid can exhibit significantly higher resistance when hot, leading to insufficient voltage reaching the starter motor. The FSM outlines procedures for 'Testing Starter Motor Voltage' and 'Voltage Drop In Starting System' (Source 50, 23, 31).\n2.  **A failing starter motor or solenoid that binds or develops high internal resistance when hot:** Internal components of the starter motor or solenoid can expand with heat, causing increased friction or electrical resistance, which reduces cranking power or results in a clicking solenoid. The FSM states that if the starter receives 9.5 volts or more but doesn't crank as it should, the starter motor could have a problem (Source 50).\n3.  **Increased mechanical resistance in the engine when hot:** Conditions such as engine overheating, pre-ignition, or dieseling can make the engine physically harder to turn over, even if the starter motor is functioning correctly. The FSM lists 'Engine Overheats (Mechanical)' as a cause for hard starting, specifically mentioning 'Wrong ignition timing' or 'Lean fuel mixture' (Source 43). 'Dieseling, Run-On Symptom' is also a possibility, often linked to engine tune, fuel, or timing (Source 5, 17).\n\nTo begin, we need to isolate whether the starter is receiving adequate power when the problem occurs.",
      "mechanic_instructions": "The engine starting normally when cold but struggling when hot points to either an electrical issue in the starting circuit that's exacerbated by heat, or increased mechanical resistance in the engine itself when hot. We need to determine if the starter motor is receiving sufficient voltage when the problem occurs.\n\n1.  Run the engine until it is at operating temperature and the hard start condition can be reproduced.\n2.  With a fully charged battery, connect your voltmeter's positive (+) lead to the large terminal on the starter solenoid where the positive (+) battery cable connects.\n3.  Connect the voltmeter's negative (–) lead to the starter motor case, ensuring good metal contact to prevent a false voltage reading.\n4.  Attempt to crank the engine for about 10 seconds and record the voltmeter reading.\n    *   **IMPORTANT:** Remove one voltmeter lead before you stop cranking the starter motor to prevent possible voltmeter damage.       "mechanic_instructions": "We need to perform a comprehensive check and adjustment of the shift cable and verify the remote control's travel. This will confirm if the transmission is fully engaging and if the remote control is providing adequate movement. Improper adjustment or insufficient travel will cause the transmission to slip and eventually fail, leading to the symptoms you're observing.\n\n1.  **Disconnect the shift cable from the transmission:** Place both the remote control shift lever and the transmission shift lever in the neutral position. Then, remove the nuts and washers from the shift cable attaching studs. (Source 2, page 20; Source 4, page 13; Source 6, page 47; Source 8, page 25; Source 25, page 13; Source 27, page 47; Source 28, page 12; Source 29, page 20; Source 35, page 25)\n2.  **Check for proper transmission shift lever positioning (manual check):** With the shift cable disconnected, manually move the transmission shift lever into the forward and reverse gear positions. The transmission is 'fully' in gear when the shift lever comes to a stop in either direction. (Source 4, page 13; Source 6, page 47; Source 14, page 24)\n    *   For Hurth transmissions, check that the shift lever is approximately 10 degrees aft of vertical in the neutral detent position. (Source 10, page 14; Source 16, page 11; Source 34, page 12; Source 46, page 12)\n    *   For Velvet Drive transmissions, ensure the poppet ball is centered in the detent hole for each F-N-R position. (Source 11, page 21; Source 21, page 48; Source 24, page 47; Source 37, page 4; Source 39, page 30; Source 41, page 26; Source 42, page 44; Source 43, page 3; Source 45, page 23; Source 48, page 44; Source 50, page 42; Source 59, page 29; Source 60, page 43)\n3.  **Measure remote control shift cable travel:** The remote control must provide a total shift cable travel (at the transmission end) of at least 2-3/4 inches (70 mm) for Velvet Drive transmissions (Source 3, page 38; Source 5, page 23; Source 12, page 45; Source 15, page 41; Source 20, page 24; Source 30, page 46; Source 33, page 40; Source 36, page 23; Source 38, page 24; Source 49, page 30; Source 54, page 46; Source 56, page 46) or 2-15/16 inches (75 mm) for some Velvet Drive 5000 series (Source 50, page 42).\n4.  **Check shift cable anchor stud position:** Verify the shift cable anchor stud is in the correct hole on the shift lever or bracket. For 5.7L MIE models, it should be in the appropriate stud hole (Source 2, page 20; Source 29, page 20). For Velvet Drive 5000 series, it should be in the lower, front hole when using Quicksilver cables (Source 21, page 48; Source 24, page 47; Source 37, page 4; Source 41, page 26; Source 42, page 44; Source 43, page 3; Source 48, page 44; Source 50, page 42; Source 58, page 24; Source 60, page 43). For Hurth transmissions, if there are two anchor holes, ensure it's in the hole marked '630' (Source 28, page 12; Source 46, page 12).\n5.  **Locate center of shift cable play (backlash):**\n    *   Ensure the remote control is in neutral.\n    *   Push in on the control cable end with enough pressure to remove play and mark position 'a' on the tube.\n    *   Pull out on the control cable end with enough effort to remove play and mark position 'b' on the tube.\n    *   Measure the distance between marks 'a' and 'b,' and mark position 'c' halfway between them. (Source 2, page 21; Source 4, page 13; Source 6, page 47; Source 8, page 25; Source 11, page 21; Source 14, page 24; Source 25, page 13; Source 27, page 47; Source 28, page 12; Source 32, page 21)\n6.  **Center cable-end play and temporarily install shift cable:** Adjust the cable barrel to align the holes in the barrel and in the cable end guide with the attaching points on the transmission. Temporarily install the shift cable without securing it. (Source 2, page 21; Source 4, page 13; Source 6, page 47; Source 8, page 25; Source 11, page 21; Source 14, page 24; Source 24, page 47; Source 28, page 12)\n7.  **Check remote control lever positioning (with cable connected):**\n    *   Place the remote control shift lever in the forward gear position. Ensure the transmission shift lever is fully in gear by holding the remote lever, carefully sliding the shift cable off its anchor points, and attempting to move the transmission lever further. It should not move further. (Source 1, page 13; Source 4, page 13)\n    *   Repeat this for the reverse gear position. (Source 1, page 13; Source 4, page 13)\n    *   For MIE models, the shift lever must be positioned over the letter 'F' for forward and 'R' for reverse, with the poppet ball centered in the detent hole. (Source 11, page 21; Source 23, page 21; Source 39, page 30)\n    *   For Velvet Drive 5000 series, the poppet ball must be centered in the correct detent hole for LH or RH propeller rotation.       "diagnostic_reasoning": "The user's observation confirms coolant leaking from around the outer edge of a corroded round plug. This is a classic symptom of a failed core plug (also known as a freeze plug or Welch plug). These plugs are designed to seal casting holes in the engine block and can corrode over time, leading to leaks. While the FSM discusses internal leaks from cracked blocks or head gaskets (Source 1, page 22), an external leak from a corroded core plug is a direct mechanical failure of the plug itself. The repair involves replacing the corroded plug. The FSM provides general instructions for draining and filling the closed cooling system (Source 1, page 28, 31), which will be necessary for this repair.",
      "mechanic_instructions": "The coolant leaking from around the outer edge of that corroded round plug confirms you have a failed core plug. These plugs are pressed into the engine block to seal casting holes and prevent coolant leaks. Over time, corrosion can compromise their seal, leading to the drip you're seeing. You'll need to replace it.\n\nHere's the procedure:\n\n1.  **Drain the Closed Cooling System:** Ensure the engine is cool. Remove the pressure cap from the heat exchanger (Source 3, page 17). Open the drain plugs on the port and starboard exhaust manifolds, and any other drain plugs for the seawater section if applicable (Source 29, page 42). For the closed cooling section, refer to 'Draining Instructions' in SECTION 6B (Source 36, page 28).\n2.  **Remove the Old Core Plug:** Use a punch and hammer to strike the lower edge of the corroded plug. Angle the punch so the plug rotates in its bore. Once it's rotated, you can usually pry it out with pliers or a screwdriver. Be careful not to damage the bore in the engine block.\n3.  **Clean the Bore:** Thoroughly clean the core plug bore in the engine block. Remove any rust, corrosion, or old sealant. The surface must be clean and smooth for the new plug to seal properly.\n4.  **Install the New Core Plug:** Apply a thin, even coat of a suitable sealant (like RTV silicone or a non-hardening sealant) around the edge of the new core plug and the bore. Position the new plug squarely in the bore. Use a core plug installation tool or a socket of the correct size (slightly smaller than the plug's diameter) and a hammer to drive the new plug in until its outer edge is flush with the engine block surface. Ensure it's seated firmly and evenly.\n5.  **Refill and Air Bleed the System:** Once the new plug is installed and the sealant has cured (follow sealant manufacturer's instructions), close all drain plugs. Refer to 'Filling Closed Cooling Section' in SECTION 6B (Source 57, page 31). This involves opening the bleeder valve on the thermostat housing, filling the heat exchanger, closing the bleeder, continuing to fill, and then running the engine at idle to purge air. Maintain coolant level in the recovery reservoir between 'ADD' and 'FULL' marks (Source 47, page 32).\n6.  **Check for Leaks:** After refilling and running the engine to operating temperature, carefully inspect the new core plug and all cooling system connections for any leaks.\n\nWhile the FSM does not contain a specific procedure for 'core plug replacement' in the provided sections, these are standard mechanical steps for this type of repair. Pay close attention to the draining and filling procedures outlined in the manual to ensure proper coolant levels and air bleeding.",
